\documentclass[11pt]{article}

    
    
    \usepackage[T1]{fontenc}
    % Nicer default font (+ math font) than Computer Modern for most use cases
    \usepackage{mathpazo}

    % Basic figure setup, for now with no caption control since it's done
    % automatically by Pandoc (which extracts ![](path) syntax from Markdown).
    \usepackage{graphicx}
    % We will generate all images so they have a width \maxwidth. This means
    % that they will get their normal width if they fit onto the page, but
    % are scaled down if they would overflow the margins.
    \makeatletter
    \def\maxwidth{\ifdim\Gin@nat@width>\linewidth\linewidth
    \else\Gin@nat@width\fi}
    \makeatother
    \let\Oldincludegraphics\includegraphics
    % Set max figure width to be 80% of text width, for now hardcoded.
    \renewcommand{\includegraphics}[1]{\Oldincludegraphics[width=.8\maxwidth]{#1}}
    % Ensure that by default, figures have no caption (until we provide a
    % proper Figure object with a Caption API and a way to capture that
    % in the conversion process - todo).
    \usepackage{caption}
    \DeclareCaptionLabelFormat{nolabel}{}
    \captionsetup{labelformat=nolabel}

    \usepackage{adjustbox} % Used to constrain images to a maximum size 
    \usepackage{xcolor} % Allow colors to be defined
    \usepackage{enumerate} % Needed for markdown enumerations to work
    \usepackage{geometry} % Used to adjust the document margins
    \usepackage{amsmath} % Equations
    \usepackage{amssymb} % Equations
    \usepackage{textcomp} % defines textquotesingle
    % Hack from http://tex.stackexchange.com/a/47451/13684:
    \AtBeginDocument{%
        \def\PYZsq{\textquotesingle}% Upright quotes in Pygmentized code
    }

    \usepackage{eurosym} % defines \euro

    \usepackage[utf8x]{inputenc} % Allow utf-8 characters in the tex document

    \usepackage{grffile} % extends the file name processing of package graphics

    \usepackage[normalem]{ulem} % ulem is needed to support strikethroughs (\sout)
                                % normalem makes italics be italics, not underlines

\def\PY@reset{\let\PY@it=\relax \let\PY@bf=\relax%
    \let\PY@ul=\relax \let\PY@tc=\relax%
    \let\PY@bc=\relax \let\PY@ff=\relax}

\def\PY@toks#1+{\ifx\relax#1\empty\else%

\def\PY@do#1{\PY@bc{\PY@tc{\PY@ul{%
    \PY@it{\PY@bf{\PY@ff{#1}}}}}}}

    \hypertarget{phase-1}{%
\section{\texorpdfstring{PHASE 1 }{PHASE 1  }}\label{phase-1}}

    \textbf{Phase 1A : DATA COLLECTION}

 Objectives: 1. Getting the data of 33 developed countries from the
dataset
(https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_World\_Factbook\_list\_of\_developed\_countries)
Note: Some countries are filtered out based on the availability of data
2. Create a dataframe of countries in terms of GDP per capita, HDI, LER
and IMR using PySpark. Where GDP: Gross Domestic Product, HDI is Human
Development Index, LER is Life Expectancy Rate and IMR is Infant
Mortality Rate

    Import the libraries

    Import the dataset from the file system

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

alternate 2. Remove the space from the name of the countries in the

other dataframes to make data same throughout 4. Add INDIA to the list
of countries so as to make an analysis with other countries.

                 \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}val} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}gdp\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}gdp\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{]}
                 \PY{n}{pop\PYZus{}val} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}pop\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}pop\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{]}
                 \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}gdp\PYZus{}perc\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{loc}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}gdp\PYZus{}perc\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{j}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}val}\PY{o}{/}\PY{n}{pop\PYZus{}val}
\end{Verbatim}


    Sum all the dataframes using weights to get a final dataframe

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}17}]:} \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}pc\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mf}{0.0001}
         \PY{n}{im\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
         \PY{n}{le\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
         \PY{n}{hdi\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{10}
\end{Verbatim}


    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}18}]:} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}dfs} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}pc\PYZus{}factor}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{final\PYZus{}gdp\PYZus{}perc\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{im\PYZus{}factor}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{final\PYZus{}im\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{le\PYZus{}factor}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{final\PYZus{}le\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{hdi\PYZus{}factor}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{final\PYZus{}hdi\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}df}\PY{p}{]}
         \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}df} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n+nb}{reduce}\PY{p}{(}\PY{k}{lambda} \PY{n}{x}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{:} \PY{n}{x}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{add}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{y}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{fill\PYZus{}value}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{final\PYZus{}pd\PYZus{}dfs}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}

    \hypertarget{phase-2}{%
\section{PHASE 2}\label{phase-2}}

    \textbf{Phase 2A : DATA PREPARATION FOR PREDICTION}

Objective:

To build prediction models to predict GDP per capita, IMR, HDI, LER, we
have to get more developmental factors from the country dataset. Below
are the developmental factors chosen based on data availability for all
countries.

\begin{enumerate}
\def\labelenumi{\arabic{enumi}.}
\tightlist
\item
  Military expenditure (\% of central government expenditure)
  (MS.MIL.XPND.ZS)
\item
  Armed forces personnel, total (MS.MIL.TOTL.P1)
\item
  Foreign direct investment, net outflows (\% of GDP)
  (BM.KLT.DINV.WD.GD.ZS)
\item
  Trade in services (\% of GDP) (BG.GSR.NFSV.GD.ZS)
\item
  Merchandise exports (current US) (TX.VAL.MRCH.CD.WT)
\item
  Agricultural land (\% of land area) (AG.LND.AGRI.ZS)
\item
  Trade (\% of GDP) (NE.TRD.GNFS.ZS)
\item
  Exports of goods and services (\% of GDP) (NE.EXP.GNFS.ZS)
\item
  Imports of goods and services (\% of GDP) (NE.IMP.GNFS.ZS)
\item
  Gross national expenditure (\% of GDP) (NE.DAB.TOTL.ZS)
\item
  Foreign direct investment, net inflows (\% of GDP)
  (BX.KLT.DINV.WD.GD.ZS)
\item
  Urban population (\% of total) (SP.URB.TOTL.IN.ZS)
\item
  Population growth (annual \%) (SP.POP.GROW)
\item
  School enrollment, primary (\% net) (SE.PRM.NENR.MA)
\item
  Pupil-teacher ratio, primary (SE.PRM.ENRL.TC.ZS)
\item
  Primary completion rate, total (SE.PRM.CMPT.ZS)
\item
  Literacy rate, adult total (\% of people ages 15 and above)
  (SE.ADT.LITR.ZS)
\item
  Unemployment, total (\% of total labor force) (modeled ILO estimate)
  (SL.UEM.TOTL.ZS)
\item
  Labor force, total (SL.TLF.TOTL.IN)
\item
  Inflation, consumer prices (annual \%) (FP.CPI.TOTL.ZG)
\item
  Services, value added (\% of GDP) (NV.SRV.TOTL.ZS)
\item
  Agriculture, forestry, and fishing, value added (\% of GDP)
  (NV.AGR.TOTL.ZS)
\end{enumerate}

    Get the data of India and build a dataframe

    Making the factors as columns and year as the rows and dividing the
factors by the dividers defined for India dataframe

                     \PY{n}{df\PYZus{}p} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}final}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{col}\PY{p}{]}
                     \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}print(df\PYZus{}p)}
                     \PY{k}{if}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{math}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{isnan}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{df\PYZus{}p}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
                         \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}print(india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}final[col])}
                         \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}final}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{col}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{first\PYZus{}NAN\PYZus{}dffill}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{df\PYZus{}p}\PY{p}{)}
                         \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}print(india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}final[col])}

    \textbf{Phase 2B : TRAIN MACHINE LEARNING MODELS TO PREDICT GDP PER
CAPITA, HDI, LER AND IMR}

Objective: 1. Find correlation of each of the four factors with the 22
selected developmental factors. 2. Selecting the top correlated factors
to train the ML model 3. Train the ML model and check accuracy

    Load the pickle file ``gdp\_randomForestRegressor\_pickle'' containing
the parameters and ML model related info. Here, we try the Random
Forest. TO find the model's quality, we use the RMSE (Root Mean Square
Error) and the R2 score. R2 is the proportion of variability that is
explained by our model. It ranges from 0-1 for maximum proportion of
variability explained

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

         \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}randomForestRegressor\PYZus{}db} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pickle}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{load}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}randomForestRegressor\PYZus{}dbfile}\PY{p}{)} 
         \PY{c+c1}{\PYZsh{}print x\PYZus{}y\PYZus{}values\PYZus{}db[\PYZsq{}x\PYZus{}val\PYZsq{}]}
         \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}forest\PYZus{}model} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}randomForestRegressor\PYZus{}db}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{forest\PYZus{}model}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}
         \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}rf\PYZus{}random} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}randomForestRegressor\PYZus{}db}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{rf\PYZus{}random}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}
         \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}rf\PYZus{}grid} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}randomForestRegressor\PYZus{}db}\PY{p}{[}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{rf\PYZus{}grid}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{]}

    We need the best parameters for our RandomForestRegressor. We try to
find them using RandomSearchCV followed by GridSearchCV. RandomSearchCV
helps to narrow down the number and range of parameters, which can then
be tested using GridSearchCV. Here we print the best params for the
model using RandomSearchCV

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}54}]:} \PY{k}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}rf\PYZus{}random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{best\PYZus{}params\PYZus{}}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\{'bootstrap': False, 'min\_samples\_leaf': 1, 'n\_estimators': 1200, 'min\_samples\_split': 2, 'max\_features': 'sqrt', 'max\_depth': 40\}

    \end{Verbatim}

    Random search helps to narrow down the range for all the
hyperparameters. This is then followed by GridSearch CV where we can
test all the combinations of the parameters unlike Random search.. Here
we find best params for the model using GridSearchCV

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

\end{Verbatim}


    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    \end{Verbatim}

    Load the pickle file ``hdi\_randomForestRegressor\_pickle'' containing

Forest. TO find the model's quality, we use the RMSE (Root Mean Square
Error) and the R2 score. R2 is the proportion of variability that is
explained by our model. It ranges from 0-1 for maximum proportion of
variability explained

    We need the best parameters for our RandomForestRegressor. We try to
find them using RandomSearchCV followed by GridSearchCV. RandomSearchCV
helps to narrow down the number and range of parameters, which can then
be tested using GridSearchCV. Here we print the best params for the
model using RandomSearchCV

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}71}]:} \PY{k}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{hdi\PYZus{}rf\PYZus{}random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{best\PYZus{}params\PYZus{}}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\{'bootstrap': False, 'min\_samples\_leaf': 1, 'n\_estimators': 1000, 'min\_samples\_split': 5, 'max\_features': 'sqrt', 'max\_depth': 110\}

    \end{Verbatim}

    Random search helps to narrow down the range for all the
hyperparameters. This is then followed by GridSearch CV where we can
test all the combinations of the parameters unlike Random search. Here
we find best params for the model using GridSearchCV

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]

    Load the pickle file ``imr\_randomForestRegressor\_pickle'' containing
the parameters and ML model related info. Here, we try the Random
Forest. TO find the model's quality, we use the RMSE (Root Mean Square
Error) and the R2 score. R2 is the proportion of variability that is
explained by our model. It ranges from 0-1 for maximum proportion of
variability explained

    We need the best parameters for our RandomForestRegressor. We try to
find them using RandomSearchCV followed by GridSearchCV. RandomSearchCV
helps to narrow down the number and range of parameters, which can then
be tested using GridSearchCV. Here we print the best params for the
model using RandomSearchCV

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}88}]:} \PY{k}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{imr\PYZus{}rf\PYZus{}random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{best\PYZus{}params\PYZus{}}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\{'bootstrap': True, 'min\_samples\_leaf': 1, 'n\_estimators': 400, 'min\_samples\_split': 2, 'max\_features': 'auto', 'max\_depth': None\}

    \end{Verbatim}

    Random search helps to narrow down the range for all the
hyperparameters. This is then followed by GridSearch CV where we can
test all the combinations of the parameters unlike Random search.. Here
we find best params for the model using GridSearchCV

    Load the pickle file ``ler\_randomForestRegressor\_pickle'' containing

Error) and the R2 score. R2 is the proportion of variability that is
explained by our model. It ranges from 0-1 for maximum proportion of
variability explained

    We need the best parameters for our RandomForestRegressor. We try to
find them using RandomSearchCV followed by GridSearchCV. RandomSearchCV
helps to narrow down the number and range of parameters, which can then
be tested using GridSearchCV. Here we print the best params for the
model using RandomSearchCV

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}105}]:} \PY{k}{print}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{ler\PYZus{}rf\PYZus{}random}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{best\PYZus{}params\PYZus{}}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
\{'bootstrap': False, 'min\_samples\_leaf': 1, 'n\_estimators': 400, 'min\_samples\_split': 5, 'max\_features': 'sqrt', 'max\_depth': 40\}

    \end{Verbatim}

    Random search helps to narrow down the range for all the
hyperparameters. This is then followed by GridSearch CV where we can
test all the combinations of the parameters unlike Random search.. Here
we find best params for the model using GridSearchCV

    \hypertarget{phase-3}{%
\section{PHASE 3}\label{phase-3}}

    Objective:

To Make prediction of GDP per capita, HDI, LER and IMR for India and all
countries and compare them and realize the condition of India in the
year 2020 and make possible predictions of future

    

    \textbf{PART 3A: PREDICTION}

    Add India to the List of Countries

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}110}]:} \PY{n}{countries\PYZus{}list}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{l+s+s1}{India}\PY{l+s+s1}{\PYZsq{}}\PY{p}{)}
          \PY{n}{countries\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}final\PYZus{}list}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}final}\PY{p}{)}
\end{Verbatim}


    This function will return an array of data which is found by taking an
average of the slopes of the columns for each row in the dataframe. The
following algorithm is followed: 1. For each column, find the slope of
the data at index i+1 and the data at index i, where i is the row number
2. Continue this process till the end of the column and find the average
of all the slopes calculated in the loop

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}111}]:} \PY{k}{def} \PY{n+nf}{get\PYZus{}timeline\PYZus{}avg\PYZus{}slope\PYZus{}array}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}pred}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
              \PY{n}{timeline\PYZus{}avg\PYZus{}slope\PYZus{}list} \PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{[}\PY{p}{]}
              \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{col} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n}{india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}pred}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{columns}\PY{p}{:}
                  \PY{n+nb}{sum} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{0}
                  \PY{n}{limit} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n+nb}{len}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}pred}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{col}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}
                  \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{i} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{limit}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}
                      \PY{n}{val} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{india\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}pred}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{col}\PY{p}{]}
                      \PY{n+nb}{sum} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{val}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{o}{+}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]} \PY{o}{\PYZhy{}} \PY{n}{val}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{i}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{)}
                  \PY{n+nb}{sum} \PY{o}{/}\PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{limit}
                  \PY{n}{timeline\PYZus{}avg\PYZus{}slope\PYZus{}list}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{append}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n+nb}{sum}\PY{p}{)}
              \PY{n}{timeline\PYZus{}avg\PYZus{}slope\PYZus{}array} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{asarray}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{timeline\PYZus{}avg\PYZus{}slope\PYZus{}list}\PY{p}{)}

    This function will return the last row data as an array

    This function (\textbf{getCountryGDPPredictionUntil2050}) will return
the GDP prediction for India and other countries until 2050 and will
return a dataframe with all data from 1995 to 2016 and predicted data
from 2017 to 2050. Here is the algorithm: 1. For each country from the
countries\_list, we get the GDP per capita dataframe and concatenate it
with the dataframe which has the developmental factors as the columns
whose correlation with GDP was more than 40\% 2. Using the dataframe, we
get the average slopes using the method defined above and also the last
row as an array using the method defined above 3. Now, we keep adding
rows by adding the last row data with the slope and updating the last
row array 4. For each row after 2016, we make the GDP per capita
prediction by function \textbf{gdp\_rf\_cv\_random.predict()} from the
Random Forest model we developed 5. The predicted GDP value is appended
to the GDP column of the \textbf{country\_prediction\_for\_plot}
dataframe along with other data calculated above to plot and visualize
in the further steps.

                  \PY{n}{n} \PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{34}
                  \PY{k}{for} \PY{n}{j} \PY{o+ow}{in} \PY{n+nb}{range}\PY{p}{(}\PY{l+m+mi}{0}\PY{p}{,}\PY{n}{n}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{:}

    This function (\textbf{getCountryLERPredictionUntil2050}) will return

                      \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}input\PYZus{}x\PYZus{}ler} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{np}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{asarray}\PY{p}{(}\PY{n}{country\PYZus{}temp\PYZus{}ler\PYZus{}df}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{values}\PY{p}{[}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{]}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{tolist}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{)}\PY{p}{)}

                      \PY{k}{if} \PY{n}{j} \PY{o}{==} \PY{n}{n}\PY{o}{\PYZhy{}}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{:}

                              \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}ler\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}pred}
                          \PY{k}{else}\PY{p}{:}
                              \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{pd}\PY{o}{.}\PY{n}{concat}\PY{p}{(}\PY{p}{[}\PY{n}{country\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}ler\PYZus{}df\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}pred}\PY{p}{]}\PY{p}{,} \PY{n}{axis}\PY{o}{=}\PY{l+m+mi}{1}\PY{p}{)}
                  \PY{n}{count} \PY{o}{+}\PY{o}{=} \PY{l+m+mi}{1}
              \PY{k}{return} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot}
\end{Verbatim}


    

    This function (\textbf{getCountryIMRPredictionUntil2050}) will return
the IMR prediction for India and other countries until 2050 and will
return a dataframe with all data from 1995 to 2016 and predicted data
from 2017 to 2050. Here is the algorithm: 1. For each country from the
countries\_list, we get the IMR dataframe and concatenate it with the
dataframe which has the developmental factors as the columns whose
correlation with IMR was more than 40\% 2. Using the dataframe, we get
the average slopes using the method defined above and also the last row
as an array using the method defined above 3. Now, we keep adding rows
by adding the last row data with the slope and updating the last row
array 4. For each row after 2016, we make the IMR prediction by function
\textbf{imr\_rf\_cv\_random.predict()} from the Random Forest model we
developed 5. The predicted IMR value is appended to the IMR column of
the \textbf{country\_prediction\_for\_plot} dataframe along with other
data calculated above to plot and visualize in the further steps.

aW50ZXJhY3RpdmUoY2hpbGRyZW49KENoZWNrYm94KHZhbHVlPVRydWUsIGRlc2NyaXB0aW9uPXUnQXVzdHJhbGlhJyksIENoZWNrYm94KHZhbHVlPVRydWUsIGRlc2NyaXB0aW9uPXUnQXVzdHLigKY=

    The \textbf{total\_development\_prediction} dataframe is defined which
is weighted sums of the predicted dataframes of all the 4 major
developmental factors : GDP, HDI, LER, IMR The factors
\textbf{gdp\_pc\_factor} refer to the multiplier for GDP dataframe,
\textbf{hdi\_pc\_factor} refer to the multiplier for HDI dataframe,
\textbf{le\_pc\_factor refer} to the multiplier for LER dataframe and
\textbf{im\_pc\_factor} refer to the multiplier for IMR dataframe. These
values are defined in phase 1

    \begin{Verbatim}[commandchars=\\\{\}]
{\color{incolor}In [{\color{incolor}133}]:} \PY{n}{total\PYZus{}development\PYZus{}prediction} \PY{o}{=} \PY{n}{gdp\PYZus{}pc\PYZus{}factor}\PY{o}{*}\PY{n}{country\PYZus{}gdp\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot} \PY{o}{+} \PYZbs{}
          \PY{n}{im\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}imr\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot} \PY{o}{+} \PYZbs{}
          \PY{n}{hdi\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}hdi\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot} \PY{o}{+} \PYZbs{}
          \PY{n}{le\PYZus{}factor} \PY{o}{*} \PY{n}{country\PYZus{}ler\PYZus{}prediction\PYZus{}for\PYZus{}plot}
\end{Verbatim}


    \textbf{Total development prediction from 1995 - 2050}

selecting the checkbox Select the year upto which you want to see the
data from the slider

    \hypertarget{conclusion}{%
\section{CONCLUSION}\label{conclusion}}

    From the plots, here are the following observations: 1. The total
development prediction of India jumps high at 3 places, one at 2025, one
at 2039 and one at 2043. This observation is also seen in the plots of
Human Development Index. This means the average standard of living of
India will be improving a lot after 2025. 2. There is a steep decrease
(\textasciitilde{} 40\%) in Infant Mortality Rate in 2025 for India.
This means that, India is gonna have improved health care system for new
born by 2025. And by 2050, India will be having the Infant Mortality
Rate almost similar to the other developed countries. 3. The Life
expectancy Rate also shows similar behavior as the Human Development
Index. This means that, this oberservation predicts major breakthroughs
in the field of Medical and Health Care by the year 2025 and 2040. 4.
The GDP per capita grows exponentially. But the number is still very low
compared to other developed countries, owing to rapid growth in
Population of the nation.

    Overall, it can be said that, India is going to experience rapid
development from the year 2025 and is gonna reach the level of the least
developed country among the 33 developed countries in 2016 (Slovak
Republic) by the year 2040; with developmental value \textasciitilde{}80
units. \textbf{Thus it can be concluded that, India wont be a developed
country in 2020. But with rapid growth from 2025, India will be
developing rapidly and will be one of the developed nations by 2040.}
